<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Heir presumptive of Austria-Hungary (1863–1914)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Franz Ferdinand" redirects here. For the band, see <a href="/wiki/Franz_Ferdinand_(band)" title="Franz Ferdinand (band)">Franz Ferdinand (band)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Franz_Ferdinand_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Franz Ferdinand (disambiguation)">Franz Ferdinand (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Archduke Franz Ferdinand Carl Ludwig Joseph Maria of Austria</b><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (18 December 1863&#160;– 28 June 1914) was the <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a> to the throne of <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand" title="Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand">assassination in Sarajevo</a> was the most immediate cause of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.
</p><p>Franz Ferdinand was the eldest son of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Karl_Ludwig_of_Austria" title="Archduke Karl Ludwig of Austria">Archduke Karl Ludwig of Austria</a>, the younger brother of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Joseph_I_of_Austria" title="Franz Joseph I of Austria">Emperor Franz Joseph I of Austria</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/Mayerling_incident" title="Mayerling incident">the death</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rudolf,_Crown_Prince_of_Austria" title="Rudolf, Crown Prince of Austria">Crown Prince Rudolf</a> in 1889 and the death of Karl Ludwig in 1896, Franz Ferdinand became the heir presumptive to the Austro-Hungarian throne. His courtship of <a href="/wiki/Sophie,_Duchess_of_Hohenberg" title="Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg">Sophie Chotek</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lady-in-waiting" title="Lady-in-waiting">lady-in-waiting</a>, caused conflict within the imperial household, and their <a href="/wiki/Morganatic_marriage" title="Morganatic marriage">morganatic marriage</a> in 1900 was only allowed after he renounced his descendants' rights to the throne. Franz Ferdinand held significant influence over the military, and in 1913 he was appointed inspector general of the Austro-Hungarian armed forces.
</p><p>On 28 June 1914, Franz Ferdinand and his wife were <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand" title="Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand">assassinated</a> in Sarajevo by the 19-year-old <a href="/wiki/Gavrilo_Princip" title="Gavrilo Princip">Gavrilo Princip</a>, a member of <a href="/wiki/Young_Bosnia" title="Young Bosnia">Young Bosnia</a>. Franz Ferdinand's assassination led to the <a href="/wiki/July_Crisis" title="July Crisis">July Crisis</a> and precipitated Austria-Hungary's <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war" title="Declaration of war">declaration of war</a> against <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbia" title="Kingdom of Serbia">Serbia</a>, which in turn triggered a series of events that eventually led&#160;&#8211;&#32;four weeks after his death&#160;&#8211;&#32;to <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Austria-Hungary's allies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Serbia's allies</a> declaring war on each other, starting World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-johnson_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Biography">Biography</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Early_life">Early life</h3></div>
<p>Franz Ferdinand was born in <a href="/wiki/Graz" title="Graz">Graz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a>, the eldest son of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Karl_Ludwig_of_Austria" title="Archduke Karl Ludwig of Austria">Archduke Karl Ludwig of Austria</a> (the younger brother of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Joseph_I_of_Austria" title="Franz Joseph I of Austria">Franz Joseph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I_of_Mexico" title="Maximilian I of Mexico">Maximilian</a>) and of his second wife, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Maria_Annunziata_of_Bourbon-Two_Sicilies" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Maria Annunziata of Bourbon-Two Sicilies">Princess Maria Annunziata of Bourbon-Two Sicilies</a>. In 1875, when he was eleven years old, his cousin <a href="/wiki/Francis_V,_Duke_of_Modena" title="Francis V, Duke of Modena">Francis V, Duke of Modena</a>, died, naming Franz Ferdinand his heir on condition that he add the name "Este" to his own. This inheritance made Franz Ferdinand one of the wealthiest men in Austria.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Heir_presumptive">Heir presumptive</h3></div>
<p>In 1889, Franz Ferdinand's life changed dramatically.  His cousin <a href="/wiki/Rudolf,_Crown_Prince_of_Austria" title="Rudolf, Crown Prince of Austria">Crown Prince Rudolf</a> committed suicide at his hunting lodge in <a href="/wiki/Mayerling_incident" title="Mayerling incident">Mayerling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brook_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brook-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This left Franz Ferdinand's father, Karl Ludwig, first in line to the throne. When Karl Ludwig died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> in 1896,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Franz Ferdinand became the heir presumptive to the Austro-Hungarian throne.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Despite this burden, he found time for travel and personal pursuits, such as his circumnavigation of the world between 1892 and 1893. After visiting India he spent time  hunting <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">kangaroos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emus</a> in Australia in 1893,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> then travelled on to <a href="/wiki/Noum%C3%A9a" title="Nouméa">Nouméa</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hebrides" title="New Hebrides">New Hebrides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After sailing across the Pacific on the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Empress_of_China_(1890)" title="RMS Empress of China (1890)">RMS <i>Empress of China</i></a> from <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> he crossed the United States, arriving at the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World&#39;s Columbian Exposition">World's Columbian Exposition</a> 1893 on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Burlington,_and_Quincy_Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Burlington, and Quincy Railroad">Chicago, Burlington, and Quincy Railroad</a> on a private <a href="/wiki/Pullman_car" class="mw-redirect" title="Pullman car">Pullman car</a> named Mascotte,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and staying at the <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Hotel_(Chicago)" title="Lexington Hotel (Chicago)">Lexington Hotel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> before continuing through to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> and returning to Europe.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg/220px-Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg/330px-Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg/440px-Franz_Ferdinand_von_%C3%96sterreich-Este_auf_Elefantenjagd_1893.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1276" data-file-height="938" /></a><figcaption>Franz Ferdinand posing in front of a killed elephant, 1893</figcaption></figure>
<p>Franz Ferdinand had a fondness for <a href="/wiki/Trophy_hunting" title="Trophy hunting">trophy hunting</a> that was excessive even by the standards of European nobility in his day.<sup id="cite_ref-Wladimir_Aichelburg_2000,_p._31_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wladimir_Aichelburg_2000,_p._31-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In his diaries he kept track of 272,511 game kills,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 5,000 of which were <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>. About 100,000 trophies were on exhibit at his Bohemian castle at <a href="/wiki/Konopi%C5%A1t%C4%9B" title="Konopiště">Konopiště</a><sup id="cite_ref-Friedrich_Weissensteiner_1978,_p._367_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich_Weissensteiner_1978,_p._367-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-p._237_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p._237-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which he also stuffed with various antiquities, his other great passion.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_Veszelits_2003,_p._106_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_Veszelits_2003,_p._106-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Military_career">Military career</h3></div>
<p>Franz Ferdinand, like most males in the ruling <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_monarchy" title="Habsburg monarchy">Habsburg</a> line, entered the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian Army</a> at a young age.  He was frequently and rapidly promoted, given the rank of <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">lieutenant</a> at age fourteen, <a href="/wiki/Captain_(armed_forces)" title="Captain (armed forces)">captain</a> at twenty-two, <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">colonel</a> at twenty-seven, and <a href="/wiki/Major_general" title="Major general">major general</a> at thirty-one.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothenburg,_G._1976._p_141_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothenburg,_G._1976._p_141-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While never receiving formal staff training, he was considered eligible for command and at one point briefly led the primarily Hungarian 9th <a href="/wiki/Hussar" title="Hussar">Hussar</a> Regiment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976120_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976120-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1898 he was given a commission "at the special disposition of His Majesty" to make inquiries into all aspects of the military services and military agencies were commanded to share their papers with him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976141_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976141-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>He also held honorary ranks in the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Navy" title="Austro-Hungarian Navy">Austro-Hungarian Navy</a>, and received the rank of <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">admiral</a> at the close of the Austro-Hungarian naval maneuvers in September 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Franz Ferdinand exerted influence on the armed forces even when he did not hold a specific command through a military <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(diplomacy)" title="Chancery (diplomacy)">chancery</a> that produced and received documents and papers on military affairs. This was headed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_Brosch_von_Aarenau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alexander Brosch von Aarenau (page does not exist)">Alexander Brosch von Aarenau</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Brosch_von_Aarenau" class="extiw" title="de:Alexander Brosch von Aarenau">de</a>&#93;</span> and eventually employed a staff of sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976141_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976141-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His authority was reinforced in 1907 when he secured the retirement of the Emperor's confidant <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Beck-Rzikowsky" title="Friedrich von Beck-Rzikowsky">Friedrich von Beck-Rzikowsky</a> as Chief of the General Staff. Beck's successor, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Conrad_von_H%C3%B6tzendorf" title="Franz Conrad von Hötzendorf">Franz Conrad von Hötzendorf</a>, was personally selected by Franz Ferdinand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976136_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976136-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Franz in 1913, as heir-presumptive to the elderly emperor, had been appointed inspector general of all the armed forces of Austria-Hungary (<i>Generalinspektor der gesamten bewaffneten Macht</i>), a position superior to that previously held by <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Albrecht,_Duke_of_Teschen" title="Archduke Albrecht, Duke of Teschen">Archduke Albrecht</a> and including presumed command in wartime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976170_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976170-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg/170px-The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="251" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg/255px-The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg/340px-The_Austro_Hungarian_Empire_Before_the_First_World_War_Q81810.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3723" data-file-height="5497" /></a><figcaption>Archduke Franz Ferdinand with his wife Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg, and their three children (from left), Prince Ernst von Hohenberg, Princess Sophie, and Maximilian, Duke of Hohenberg, in 1910</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1894, Franz Ferdinand met Countess <a href="/wiki/Sophie,_Duchess_of_Hohenberg" title="Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg">Sophie Chotek</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lady-in-waiting" title="Lady-in-waiting">lady-in-waiting</a> to <a href="/wiki/Princess_Isabella_of_Croy" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Isabella of Croy">Archduchess Isabella</a>, wife of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Friedrich,_Duke_of_Teschen" title="Archduke Friedrich, Duke of Teschen">Archduke Friedrich, Duke of Teschen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Radziwill_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radziwill-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Franz began to visit Archduke Friedrich's villa in Pressburg (now <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>), and in turn Sophie wrote to Franz Ferdinand during his convalescence from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Lo%C5%A1inj" title="Lošinj">Lošinj</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a>. They kept their relationship a secret,<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2007_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2007-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> until it was discovered by Isabella.
</p><p>To be eligible to marry a member of the imperial House of Habsburg, one had to be a member of one of the reigning or formerly reigning dynasties of Europe. The Choteks were not one of these families. Deeply in love, Franz Ferdinand refused to consider marrying anyone else. Finally, in 1899, Emperor Franz Joseph agreed to permit Franz Ferdinand to marry Sophie, provided that the marriage would be <a href="/wiki/Morganatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Morganatic">morganatic</a> and that their descendants would not have succession rights to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-brook_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brook-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sophie would not share her husband's rank, title, <a href="/wiki/Order_of_precedence" title="Order of precedence">precedence</a>, or privileges, nor would she normally appear in public beside him. She would not be allowed to ride in the royal carriage or sit in the royal box in theaters.<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2007_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2007-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The wedding took place on 1 July 1900, at Reichstadt (now <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%A1kupy" title="Zákupy">Zákupy</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>; Franz Joseph did not attend the ceremony, nor did Franz Ferdinand's brothers or any other archduke.<sup id="cite_ref-brook_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brook-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The only members of the imperial family present were Franz Ferdinand's stepmother, <a href="/wiki/Infanta_Maria_Theresa_of_Portugal" title="Infanta Maria Theresa of Portugal">Princess Maria Theresa of Braganza</a>, and her two daughters. Upon the marriage, Sophie was given the title "Princess of Hohenberg" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Fürstin von Hohenberg</i></span>) with the style "Her Serene Highness" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Ihre Durchlaucht</i></span>). In 1909, she was given the more senior title "Duchess of Hohenberg" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Herzogin von Hohenberg</i></span>) with the style "Her Highness" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Ihre Hoheit</i></span>). This raised her status considerably, but she was still required to yield precedence at court to all the archduchesses. Whenever a function required the couple to assemble with the other members of the imperial family, Sophie had to stand far down the line, separated from her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-Meyer2007_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2007-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Franz Ferdinand's children were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Princess_Sophie_of_Hohenberg" title="Princess Sophie of Hohenberg">Princess Sophie of Hohenberg</a> (1901–1990), married Count Friedrich von Nostitz-Rieneck (1891–1973)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maximilian,_Duke_of_Hohenberg" title="Maximilian, Duke of Hohenberg">Maximilian, Duke of Hohenberg</a> (1902–1962), married Countess Elisabeth von Waldburg zu Wolfegg und Waldsee (1904–1993)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prince_Ernst_of_Hohenberg" title="Prince Ernst of Hohenberg">Prince Ernst of Hohenberg</a> (1904–1954), married Marie-Therese Wood (1910–1985)</li>
<li>Stillborn son (1908), buried in <a href="/wiki/Artstetten_Castle" title="Artstetten Castle">Artstetten Castle</a>, near his parents<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Franz Ferdinand and Sophie visited England in the autumn of 1913, spending a week with <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">Queen Mary</a> at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a> before going to stay for another week with the <a href="/wiki/William_Cavendish-Bentinck,_6th_Duke_of_Portland" title="William Cavendish-Bentinck, 6th Duke of Portland">Duke of Portland</a> at <a href="/wiki/Welbeck_Abbey" title="Welbeck Abbey">Welbeck Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire" title="Nottinghamshire">Nottinghamshire</a>, where they arrived on 22 November. He attended a service at the local Catholic church in <a href="/wiki/Worksop" title="Worksop">Worksop</a>.  Franz Ferdinand and the Duke of Portland went game shooting on the Welbeck estate when, according to Portland's memoirs, <i>Men, Women and Things</i>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>One of the loaders fell down. This caused both barrels of the gun he was carrying to be discharged, the shot passing within a few feet of the archduke and myself. I have often wondered whether the Great War might not have been averted, or at least postponed, had the archduke met his death there and not in Sarajevo the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Assassination">Assassination</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand" title="Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand">Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg/220px-Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg/330px-Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg/440px-Minutos_previos_al_atentado_en_Sarajevo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1151" data-file-height="822" /></a><figcaption>As described by contemporary Spanish magazine <i>El Mundo Gráfico</i>: "The moment when the Austrian archdukes, following the first attempt against their lives, arrived at the City Council (of Sarajevo), where they were received by the mayor and the municipal corporation."</figcaption></figure>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1034237262">.mw-parser-output .stack{box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .stack>div{margin:1px;overflow:hidden}@media all and (min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-left{float:left;clear:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-right{float:right;clear:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-left{float:left;clear:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-clear-right{float:right;clear:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-left{float:left;margin-right:1em}.mw-parser-output .stack-margin-right{float:right;margin-left:1em}}</style><div class="stack mw-stack stack-left"><div><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg/170px-FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg/255px-FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg/340px-FranzFerdinand_uniform.jpg 2x" data-file-width="833" data-file-height="1090" /></a><figcaption>Franz Ferdinand's blood-stained uniform</figcaption></figure>
</div></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:FranzFerdinandCar.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/FranzFerdinandCar.jpg/220px-FranzFerdinandCar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/FranzFerdinandCar.jpg/330px-FranzFerdinandCar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/FranzFerdinandCar.jpg/440px-FranzFerdinandCar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption>The 1910 <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A4f_%26_Stift" title="Gräf &amp; Stift">Gräf &amp; Stift</a> <i>Bois de Boulogne</i> <a href="/wiki/Phaeton_body" title="Phaeton body">phaeton</a> automobile in which Archduke Franz Ferdinand and his wife were assassinated. It is now displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Military_History,_Vienna" title="Museum of Military History, Vienna">Museum of Military History</a> in Vienna</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Latin_Bridge_02_(22757259615).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Latin bridge (prev. Princip bridge) in Sarajevo. Across the bridge is a street of several grayish houses not more than four stories high." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg/220px-Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg/330px-Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg/440px-Latin_Bridge_02_%2822757259615%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1676" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_Bridge" title="Latin Bridge">Latin Bridge</a> near the assassination site</figcaption></figure>
<p>On Sunday, 28 June 1914, at about 10:45 am, Franz Ferdinand and his wife were assassinated in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_rule_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Austro-Hungarian rule in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Austro-Hungarian province of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>. The perpetrator was 19-year-old <a href="/wiki/Gavrilo_Princip" title="Gavrilo Princip">Gavrilo Princip</a>, a member of <a href="/wiki/Young_Bosnia" title="Young Bosnia">Young Bosnia</a> and one of a group of assassins organized and armed by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Hand_(Serbia)" title="Black Hand (Serbia)">Black Hand</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Earlier in the day, the couple had been attacked by <a href="/wiki/Nedeljko_%C4%8Cabrinovi%C4%87" title="Nedeljko Čabrinović">Nedeljko Čabrinović</a>, also a Young Bosnia conspirator, who had thrown a grenade at their car. However, the bomb detonated behind them, injuring the occupants in the following car. On arriving at the Governor's residence, Franz asked "So you welcome your guests with bombs!"<sup id="cite_ref-beyer146_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beyer146-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After a short rest at the Governor's residence, the royal couple insisted on seeing all those who had been injured by the bomb at the local hospital. However, no one told the drivers that the itinerary had been changed. When the error was discovered, the drivers had to turn around. As the cars backed down the street and onto a side street, the line of cars stalled. At this time, Princip was sitting at a cafe across the street. He instantly seized his opportunity and walked across the street and shot the royal couple.<sup id="cite_ref-beyer146_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beyer146-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He first shot Sophie in the abdomen and then shot Franz Ferdinand in the neck. Franz leaned over his crying wife. He was still alive when witnesses arrived to render aid.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His dying words to Sophie were, "Don't die darling, live for our children."<sup id="cite_ref-beyer146_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beyer146-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Princip's weapon was the pocket-sized <a href="/wiki/FN_Model_1910" title="FN Model 1910">FN Model 1910</a> pistol chambered for the <a href="/wiki/.380_ACP" title=".380 ACP">.380 ACP</a> cartridge provided him by Serbian Army <a href="/wiki/Military_Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Intelligence">Military Intelligence</a> <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant-Colonel" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant-Colonel">Lieutenant-Colonel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Hand_(Serbia)" title="Black Hand (Serbia)">Black Hand</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Dragutin_Dimitrijevi%C4%87" title="Dragutin Dimitrijević">Dragutin Dimitrijević</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Franz Ferdinand's aides attempted to undo his coat but realized they needed scissors to cut it open: the outer lapel had been sewn to the inner front of the jacket for a smoother fit to improve his appearance to the public.  Whether or not as a result of this obstacle, his wound could not be attended to in time to save him, and he died within minutes. Sophie also died en route to the hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A detailed account of the shooting can be found in <i>Sarajevo</i> by Joachim Remak:<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>One bullet pierced Franz Ferdinand's neck while the other pierced Sophie's abdomen.&#160;... As the car was reversing (to go back to the Governor's residence because the entourage thought the Imperial couple were unhurt) a thin streak of blood shot from the Archduke's mouth onto Count Harrach's right cheek (he was standing on the car's running board). Harrach drew out a handkerchief to still the gushing blood. The Duchess, seeing this, called: "For Heaven's sake! What happened to you?" and sank from her seat, her face falling between her husband's knees.
</p><p>Harrach and Potoriek&#160;... thought she had fainted&#160;... only her husband seemed to have an instinct for what was happening. Turning to his wife despite the bullet in his neck, Franz Ferdinand pleaded: "<i>Sopherl! Sopherl! Sterbe nicht! Bleibe am Leben für unsere Kinder!</i>&#160;– Sophie dear! Don't die! Stay alive for our children!" Having said this, he seemed to sag down himself. His plumed hat&#160;... fell off; many of its green feathers were found all over the car floor. Count Harrach seized the Archduke by the uniform collar to hold him up. He asked "<i>Leiden Eure Kaiserliche Hoheit sehr?</i>&#160;– Is Your Imperial Highness suffering very badly?"  "<i>Es ist nichts.</i>&#160;– It is nothing." said the Archduke in a weak but audible voice. He seemed to be losing consciousness during his last few minutes, but, his voice growing steadily weaker, he repeated the phrase perhaps six or seven times more.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Death_rattle" title="Death rattle">rattle</a> began to issue from his throat, which subsided as the car drew in front of the Konak bersibin (Town Hall).
Despite several doctors' efforts, the Archduke died shortly after being carried into the building while his beloved wife was almost certainly dead from internal bleeding before the motorcade reached the Konak.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The assassinations, along with the <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarism</a> of <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Imperial Germany</a> and the alliance system all contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Origins of World War I">origins of World War I</a>, which began a month after Franz Ferdinand's death, with Austria-Hungary's declaration of war against Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson198956_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson198956-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The assassination of Franz Ferdinand is considered the most immediate cause of World War&#160;I.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG/220px-Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG/330px-Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG/440px-Artstetten_-_Schloss_mit_Kirche.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4425" data-file-height="2950" /></a><figcaption>Artstetten Castle with family crypt under the forecourt of the castle church</figcaption></figure>
<p>After his death, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_Austria" title="Charles I of Austria">Archduke Karl</a> became the heir presumptive of Austria-Hungary. Franz Ferdinand was buried with his wife Sophie in <a href="/wiki/Artstetten_Castle" title="Artstetten Castle">Artstetten Castle</a>, Austria.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Character">Character</h2></div>
<p>The German historian Michael Freund described Franz Ferdinand as "a man of uninspired energy, dark in appearance and emotion, who radiated an aura of strangeness and cast a shadow of violence and recklessness&#160;... a true personality amidst the amiable inanity that characterized Austrian society at this time."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As his sometime admirer <a href="/wiki/Karl_Kraus_(writer)" title="Karl Kraus (writer)">Karl Kraus</a> put it, "he was not one who would greet you&#160;... he felt no compulsion to reach out for the unexplored region which the Viennese call their heart."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His relations with Emperor Franz Joseph were tense; the emperor's personal servant recalled in his memoirs that "thunder and lightning always raged when they had their discussions."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The commentaries and orders which the heir to the throne wrote as margin notes to the documents of the Imperial central commission for architectural conservation (where he was Protector) reveal what can be described as "choleric conservatism."<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Italian historian Leo Valiani provided the following description.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Francis Ferdinand was a prince of absolutist inclinations, but he had certain intellectual gifts and undoubted moral earnestness. One of his projects – though because of his impatient, suspicious, almost hysterical temperament, his commitment to it, and the methods by which he proposed to bring it about, often changed – was to consolidate the structure of the state and the authority and popularity of the Crown, on which he saw clearly that the fate of the dynasty depended, by abolishing, if not the dominance of the German Austrians, which he wished to maintain for military reasons, though he wanted to diminish it in the civil administration, certainly the far more burdensome sway of the Magyars over the Slav and Romanian nationalities which in 1848–49 had saved the dynasty in armed combat with the Hungarian revolution. Baron Margutti <small>(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_von_Margutti" class="extiw" title="de:Albert von Margutti">de</a>)</small>, Francis Joseph's aide-de-camp, was told by Francis Ferdinand in 1895 and – with a remarkable consistency in view of the changes that took place in the intervening years – again in 1913, that the introduction of the dual system in 1867 had been disastrous and that, when he ascended the throne, he intended to re-establish strong central government: this objective, he believed, could be attained only by the simultaneous granting of far-reaching administrative autonomy to all the nationalities of the monarchy. In a letter of February 1, 1913, to <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Berchtold" title="Leopold Berchtold">Berchtold</a>, the Foreign Minister, in which he gave his reasons for not wanting war with Serbia, Franz Ferdinand said that "irredentism in our country&#160;... will cease immediately if our Slavs are given a comfortable, fair and good life" instead of being trampled on (as they were being trampled on by the Hungarians). It must have been this which caused Berchtold, in a character sketch of Francis Ferdinand written ten years after his death, to say that, if he had succeeded to the throne, he would have tried to replace the dual system by a supranational federation.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>After the tragic assassination of Franz Ferdinand and Sophie in 1914, Marie Valerie, the daughter of Emperor Franz Joseph, observed that her father had placed more trust in the newly designated heir, his grandnephew Archduke Charles. The emperor confessed to his daughter about the assassination, stating: "For me, it is a relief from a great worry."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Political_views">Political views</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Greater_austria.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Greater_austria.png/310px-Greater_austria.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="265" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Greater_austria.png/465px-Greater_austria.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Greater_austria.png/620px-Greater_austria.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1280" /></a><figcaption>Map of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_Greater_Austria" title="United States of Greater Austria">federalization of Austria-Hungary</a> planned by Archduke Franz Ferdinand, the member states with separate governments</figcaption></figure>
<p>"The three cornerstones of Ferdinand’s political conviction were clericalism, anti-democratic views, and anti-Hungarianism,” and the basis of his worldview was that “politics is a matter only for the ruler, while the people, the masses have to obey.” Franz Ferdinand often complained that in Hungary, the glorification of revolutionary hero <a href="/wiki/Lajos_Kossuth" title="Lajos Kossuth">Lajos Kossuth</a>, the decline of the monarchical principle, and the dominance of the Freemasons and the Jewish people was prevalent.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Historians have disagreed on how to characterize the political philosophies of Franz Ferdinand, some attributing generally liberal views on the empire's nationalities while others have emphasized his dynastic centralism, Catholic conservatism, and tendency to clash with other leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothenburg,_G._1976._p_141_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothenburg,_G._1976._p_141-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>He advocated granting greater autonomy to ethnic groups within the Empire and addressing their grievances, especially the Czechs in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> and the south Slavic peoples in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnia">Bosnia</a>, who had been left out of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Compromise_of_1867" title="Austro-Hungarian Compromise of 1867">Austro-Hungarian Compromise of 1867</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yet his feelings towards the Hungarians were less generous, often described as antipathy. For example, in 1904 he wrote that "The Hungarians are all rabble, regardless of whether they are minister or duke, cardinal or burgher, peasant, hussar, domestic servant, or revolutionary", and he regarded even <a href="/wiki/Istv%C3%A1n_Tisza" title="István Tisza">István Tisza</a> as a revolutionary and "patented traitor".<sup id="cite_ref-Köpeczi_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Köpeczi-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He regarded <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_nationalism" title="Hungarian nationalism">Hungarian nationalism</a> as a revolutionary threat to the Habsburg dynasty and reportedly became angry when officers of the 9th Hussars Regiment (which he commanded) spoke Hungarian in his presence&#160;– despite the fact that it was the official regimental language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976120_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976120-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He further regarded the Hungarian branch of the Dual Monarchy's army, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hungarian_Honv%C3%A9d" title="Royal Hungarian Honvéd">Honvédség</a>, as an unreliable and potentially threatening force within the empire, complaining at the Hungarians' failure to provide funds for the joint army<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976147_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976147-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and opposing the formation of artillery units within the Hungarian forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976133_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976133-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>He also advocated a cautious approach towards <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbia" title="Kingdom of Serbia">Serbia</a>&#160;– repeatedly locking horns with <a href="/wiki/Franz_Conrad_von_H%C3%B6tzendorf" title="Franz Conrad von Hötzendorf">Franz Conrad von Hötzendorf</a>, Vienna's hard-line Chief of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_General_Staff" title="Austro-Hungarian General Staff">Austro-Hungarian General Staff</a>, warning that harsh treatment of Serbia would bring Austria-Hungary into open conflict with <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, to the ruin of both empires.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>He was disappointed when Austria-Hungary failed to act as a great power, such as during the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> in 1900. Other nations, including, in his description, "dwarf states like Belgium and Portugal",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976136_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothenburg1976136-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> had soldiers stationed in China, but Austria-Hungary did not. However, Austria-Hungary did participate in the <a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">Eight-Nation Alliance</a> to suppress the Boxers, and sent soldiers as part of the "international relief force".
</p><p>Franz Ferdinand was a prominent and influential supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Navy" title="Austro-Hungarian Navy">Austro-Hungarian Navy</a> in a time when sea power was not a priority in Austrian foreign policy and the Navy was relatively little known or supported by the public. After his assassination in 1914, the Navy honoured Franz Ferdinand and his wife with a <a href="/wiki/Lying_in_state" title="Lying in state">lying in state</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/SMS_Viribus_Unitis" title="SMS Viribus Unitis">SMS <i>Viribus Unitis</i></a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Commemorations">Commemorations</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The bluish-tinted stamp shows Sophia, duchess of Hohenberg on the left, and Franz Ferdinand on the right. The stamp is titled &quot;Militärpost&quot; (&quot;Military Mail&quot;) at the top, and the date of the couple&#39;s deaths at the bottom." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg/170px-Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="110" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg/255px-Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg/340px-Franzferdinand40hel1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1075" data-file-height="698" /></a><figcaption>Commemorative postage stamp (Austria-Hungary)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Archduke Franz Ferdinand and his <a href="/wiki/Artstetten_Castle" title="Artstetten Castle">Castle of Artstetten</a> were selected as a main motif for the Austrian 10 euro <a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Austria)#2004_coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Austria)">The Castle of Artstetten commemorative coin</a>, minted on 13 October 2004. The reverse shows the entrance to the crypt of the Hohenberg family. There are two portraits below, showing Archduke Franz Ferdinand and his wife Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Titles,_styles,_honours_and_arms"><span id="Titles.2C_styles.2C_honours_and_arms"></span>Titles, styles, honours and arms</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Titles_and_styles">Titles and styles</h3></div>
<ul><li>18 December 1863&#160;– 20 November 1875: <i>His Imperial and Royal Highness</i> Archduke and Prince Franz Ferdinand of Austria, Royal Prince of Hungary, Bohemia and Croatia<sup id="cite_ref-harmonische_Wahlkapitulation_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harmonische_Wahlkapitulation-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>20 November 1875&#160;– 28 June 1914: <i>His Imperial and Royal Highness</i> Franz Ferdinand, Archduke of Austria-Este<sup id="cite_ref-HofUndStaat_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HofUndStaat-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Honours_and_awards">Honours and awards</h3></div>

</p>

</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1184024115">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 25em;">

Friedrich Weissensteiner, <i>75 Jahre aus bewegter Zeit: Lebenserinnerungen eines österreichischen Staatsmannes</i>, Veröffentlichungen der Kommission für neuere Geschichte Österreichs 64, Vienna: Böhlau, 1978, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-205-08565-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-205-08565-2">978-3-205-08565-2</a>, p. 367 <span class="languageicon">(in German)</span>: <i>"Konopischt&#160;... das einst dem Erzherzoge Franz Ferdinand gehört hatte. Das Schloß ist voller Jagdtrophäen"</i> - "Konopiště&#160;... which once belonged to Archduke Franz Ferdinand. The castle is full of hunting trophies."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Thomas_Veszelits_2003,_p._106-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Thomas_Veszelits_2003,_p._106_19-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Veszelits, <i>Prag</i>, HB-Bildatlas 248,  Ostfildern: HB, 2003, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-616-06152-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-616-06152-8">978-3-616-06152-8</a>, p. 106. <span class="languageicon">(in German)</span>: <i>"Jagdtrophäen, Waffen aus drei Jahrhunderten und Kunstschätze füllten die Räume"</i> – "Hunting trophies, weapons dating to three centuries, and art treasures filled the rooms."</span>
